Output:  We want to find the derivative of the polynomial x⁴ − 2x² + 1 with respect to x — in other words, how fast this expression changes as x changes.

We differentiate term by term: the derivative of x⁴ is 4x³, the derivative of −2x² is −4x, and the derivative of the constant 1 is 0.

Combining those results gives the final answer: **d/dx(x⁴ − 2x² + 1) = 4x³ − 4x**.

## sonnet-4-6 (claude_cli) — backend=sympy — solver=false — run 1

   --param backend=sympy \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'differentiate x**4 - 2*x**2 + 1' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 111 tokens, 6494ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (446 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 446 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  We want to find the derivative of f(x) = x⁴ − 2x² + 1 with respect to x — that is, the rate of change of this polynomial at any point x.

The derivative is taken term by term, using the power rule: d/dx[xⁿ] = n·xⁿ⁻¹, and the fact that the derivative of a constant is zero.

**Term 1:** d/dx[x⁴] = 4·x³

**Term 2:** d/dx[−2x²] = −2 · 2·x¹ = −4x

**Term 3:** d/dx[1] = 0

Adding the results: f'(x) = 4x³ − 4x + 0

**Final answer: f'(x) = 4x³ − 4x**
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 6495ms
Log:     /Users/mac-wen/.spl/logs/symbolic_math-claude_cli-claude-sonnet-4-6-20260620-121240.md

## sonnet-4-6 (claude_cli) — backend=sympy — solver=false — run 1

_Tier: T0 | Problem ID: `p011` | Host: `mac-wens-Mac-mini.local`_

```bash
(spl123) $ spl3 run cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl --kernel --llm claude_cli \
   --param problem="simplify the rational expression (x**2 - 1) / (x - 1)" \
   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sympy \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'simplify the rational expression (x**2 - 1) / (x - 1)' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 173 tokens, 7705ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (694 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 694 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  We want to simplify the fraction (x² − 1) / (x − 1) by canceling any factors shared by the numerator and denominator.

**Step 1 — Factor the numerator.** The numerator x² − 1 is a difference of two squares, and the difference-of-squares identity says a² − b² = (a + b)(a − b). Setting a = x and b = 1 gives x² − 1 = (x + 1)(x − 1).

**Step 2 — Rewrite the expression.** Substituting that factored form in:

(x² − 1) / (x − 1) = (x + 1)(x − 1) / (x − 1)

**Step 3 — Cancel the common factor.** The factor (x − 1) appears in both numerator and denominator. As long as x ≠ 1 (we cannot divide by zero), we can cancel it:

(x + 1)(x − 1) / (x − 1) = x + 1

**Final answer: x + 1, valid for x ≠ 1.**
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 7706ms
Log:     /Users/mac-wen/.spl/logs/symbolic_math-claude_cli-claude-sonnet-4-6-20260620-121254.md
```

## sonnet-4-6 (claude_cli) — backend=sympy — solver=false — run 1

_Tier: T1 | Problem ID: `p002` | Host: `mac-wens-Mac-mini.local`_

```bash
(spl123) $ spl3 run cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl --kernel --llm claude_cli \
   --param problem="expand (x+1)**2, then factor the expanded form" \
   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sympy \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'expand (x+1)**2, then factor the expanded form' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 201 tokens, 9063ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (807 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 807 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  **The problem** asks us to first multiply out (x+1)² into a polynomial, then reverse the process and write that polynomial as a product of factors.

**Step 1 — Expand.** (x+1)² means (x+1)(x+1). Multiply every term in the first factor by every term in the second:

- x · x = x²
- x · 1 = x
- 1 · x = x
- 1 · 1 = 1

Collecting those four pieces: x² + x + x + 1 = **x² + 2x + 1**.

**Step 2 — Factor x² + 2x + 1.** We look for two numbers that multiply to 1 (the constant) and add to 2 (the middle coefficient). Those numbers are 1 and 1, since 1 × 1 = 1 and 1 + 1 = 2. So we write x² + 2x + 1 = (x + 1)(x + 1) = **(x + 1)²**.

**Final answer:** Expanding gives x² + 2x + 1, and factoring that expanded form gives back **(x + 1)²** — the same expression we started with, which confirms both steps are correct.
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 9064ms
Log:     /Users/mac-wen/.spl/logs/symbolic_math-claude_cli-claude-sonnet-4-6-20260620-121310.md
```

Status:  complete
Output:  Here's a clear explanation for the student:

---

We want to find where the slope of the curve **3x³ - x** equals zero — first by differentiating, then factoring, then solving.

**Step 1 — Differentiate:** Taking the derivative gives **9x² - 1**.

**Step 2 — Factor:** The expression 9x² - 1 is a difference of squares, which factors as **(3x - 1)(3x + 1)**.

**Step 3 — Solve:** Setting each factor equal to zero gives the two solutions.

**Final answer: x = -1/3 and x = 1/3** — these are the points where the original curve has a horizontal tangent.

## sonnet-4-6 (claude_cli) — backend=sympy — solver=false — run 1

_Tier: T1 | Problem ID: `p003` | Host: `mac-wens-Mac-mini.local`_

```bash
(spl123) $ spl3 run cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl --kernel --llm claude_cli \
   --param problem="differentiate 3*x**3-x, then factor if needed, finally solve for x" \
   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sympy \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'differentiate 3*x**3-x, then factor if needed, finally solve for x' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 137 tokens, 7874ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (548 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 548 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  We want to find the derivative of f(x) = 3x³ − x, factor that derivative, then find the values of x where it equals zero.

**Step 1 — Differentiate using the power rule (d/dx[xⁿ] = n·xⁿ⁻¹):**
- d/dx[3x³] = 3·3·x² = 9x²
- d/dx[−x] = −1

So **f′(x) = 9x² − 1**.

**Step 2 — Factor 9x² − 1:**
Recognizing this as a difference of squares, (3x)² − 1² = **(3x + 1)(3x − 1)**.

**Step 3 — Solve (3x + 1)(3x − 1) = 0:**
- 3x + 1 = 0 → x = −1/3
- 3x − 1 = 0 → x = 1/3

**Final answer: x = ±1/3** (these are the critical points where the slope of f is zero).
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 7875ms
Log:     /Users/mac-wen/.spl/logs/symbolic_math-claude_cli-claude-sonnet-4-6-20260620-121327.md
```

Output:  The problem asks you to take the expression (x−2)³, expand it into standard polynomial form, find its derivative, simplify and factor the derivative, then find where that derivative equals zero.

**Step 1 — Expand (x−2)³.**
First compute (x−2)² = x² − 4x + 4, then multiply by (x−2):
(x² − 4x + 4)(x−2) = x³ − 2x² − 4x² + 8x + 4x − 8 = **x³ − 6x² + 12x − 8**.

**Step 2 — Differentiate.**

**Step 3 — Simplify.**

**Step 4 — Factor.**
x² − 4x + 4 is a perfect square trinomial, so 3(x² − 4x + 4) = **3(x − 2)²**.

**Step 5 — Solve 3(x−2)² = 0.**
Divide both sides by 3: (x−2)² = 0, so x−2 = 0, giving **x = 2**.

The final answer is **x = 2** (a repeated root, which geometrically means the derivative just touches zero at this point without changing sign — the original cubic has an inflection point there).

Status:  complete
Output:  **Partial Fraction Decomposition of 1/(x² − 1)**

The goal is to rewrite the single fraction 1/(x²−1) as a sum of simpler fractions, each with a linear denominator.

**Step 1 — Factor the denominator.**
x² − 1 = (x − 1)(x + 1), so the expression becomes 1/((x−1)(x+1)).

**Step 2 — Write the partial fraction template.**
Two distinct linear factors give two unknowns: 1/((x−1)(x+1)) = A/(x−1) + B/(x+1).

**Step 3 — Clear denominators** by multiplying both sides by (x−1)(x+1):
1 = A(x+1) + B(x−1).

**Step 4 — Solve for A** by substituting x = 1 (kills the B term):
1 = A(2) + B(0), so **A = 1/2**.

**Step 5 — Solve for B** by substituting x = −1 (kills the A term):
1 = A(0) + B(−2), so **B = −1/2**.

**Final answer:**

$$\frac{1}{x^2-1} = \frac{1}{2(x-1)} - \frac{1}{2(x+1)}$$

   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'differentiate exp(x) and simplify it if necessary' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 211 tokens, 10177ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (844 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 844 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  **Problem:** Find the derivative of f(x) = eˣ with respect to x, and simplify if possible.

**Step 1 — Apply the limit definition of the derivative:**

$$f'(x) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{e^{x+h} - e^x}{h}$$

**Step 2 — Factor out eˣ** (since it doesn't depend on h):

$$= \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{e^x \cdot e^h - e^x}{h} = e^x \cdot \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{e^h - 1}{h}$$

**Step 3 — Evaluate the remaining limit.** Expand eʰ as its Taylor series: eʰ = 1 + h + h²/2! + h³/3! + ···, so:

$$\frac{e^h - 1}{h} = \frac{h + h^2/2! + h^3/3! + \cdots}{h} = 1 + \frac{h}{2!} + \frac{h^2}{3!} + \cdots$$

As h → 0, every term after the 1 vanishes, giving **lim(h→0) (eʰ − 1)/h = 1**.

**Step 4 — Substitute back:**

$$f'(x) = e^x \cdot 1 = e^x$$

**No simplification is needed** — the function and its derivative are identical.

**Final answer:** d/dx [eˣ] = **eˣ**

(spl123) $ spl3 run cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl --kernel --llm claude_cli \
   --param problem="find the limit of sin(x) divided by x as x approaches 0" \
   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sympy \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the limit of sin(x) divided by x as x approaches 0' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 278 tokens, 26528ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1115 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1115 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  We want to find the value that sin(x)/x *approaches* as x gets arbitrarily close to 0 — it isn't defined *at* x = 0 (division by zero), but the limit may still exist.

**Step 1 — Geometric setup (assume x ∈ (0, π/2)):** On a unit circle, draw angle x and identify three nested regions sharing that angle: triangle OAB (one leg = sin x), circular sector OAB, and outer right triangle OAC (one leg = tan x); their areas are sin(x)/2, x/2, and tan(x)/2 respectively.

**Step 2 — Area inequality:** Because triangle OAB ⊂ sector OAB ⊂ triangle OAC, we have sin(x)/2 ≤ x/2 ≤ tan(x)/2.

**Step 3 — Rearrange:** Multiply by 2 and divide by sin(x) > 0 to get 1 ≤ x/sin(x) ≤ 1/cos(x); take reciprocals (reversing both inequalities) to obtain **cos(x) ≤ sin(x)/x ≤ 1**.

**Step 4 — Apply the Squeeze Theorem:** As x → 0⁺, cos(x) → cos(0) = 1, so sin(x)/x is squeezed between cos(x) and 1, both tending to 1; therefore lim_{x→0⁺} sin(x)/x = 1.

**Step 5 — Left-hand limit:** Since sin(−x)/(−x) = sin(x)/x, the function is even, so the left-hand limit equals the right-hand limit = 1.

**Final answer: lim_{x→0} sin(x)/x = 1.**

Status:  complete
Output:  The problem asks us to approximate sin(x) as a polynomial near x = 0, including only terms up to the 5th power of x. The symbolic engine computed the Taylor series expansion directly: `series(sin(x), x=0, deg≤5) = x - x³/6 + x⁵/120`. Each term comes from the pattern of derivatives of sin(x) evaluated at 0 — the odd-powered terms survive while all even-powered terms vanish. So the final answer is:

**sin(x) ≈ x − x³/6 + x⁵/120**

This polynomial gets remarkably close to sin(x) for small values of x, and the approximation improves as you include higher-degree terms.

- f′(x) = cos(x) → f′(0) = 1
- f″(x) = −sin(x) → f″(0) = 0

- f⁽⁵⁾(x) = cos(x) → f⁽⁵⁾(0) = 1

**Step 3 — Plug into the formula.** sin(x) ≈ 0 + (1)x/1! + (0)x²/2! + (−1)x³/3! + (0)x⁴/4! + (1)x⁵/5!

**Step 4 — Simplify, dropping the zero terms and computing the factorials** (3! = 6, 5! = 120):

$$\boxed{\sin(x) \approx x - \frac{x^3}{6} + \frac{x^5}{120}}$$

```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl

IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)

Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'simplify sin(x)**2 + cos(x)**2 using trigonometric identities' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 266 tokens, 10171ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1067 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1067 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  **Problem:** We want to find the numerical value that `sin²(x) + cos²(x)` always equals, for any angle `x`.

**Step 1 — Set up the unit circle.** Draw a circle of radius 1 centered at the origin. For any angle `x`, the point where the terminal ray meets the circle has coordinates `(cos(x), sin(x))` — that is the definition of cosine and sine.

**Step 2 — Apply the Pythagorean theorem.** That point has horizontal distance `cos(x)` from the origin and vertical distance `sin(x)`. The straight-line distance from the origin to the point is the radius, which is exactly `1`.

**Step 3 — Write the equation.** The Pythagorean theorem says (horizontal)² + (vertical)² = (distance)², so:

```
cos²(x) + sin²(x) = 1²
```

**Step 4 — Reorder to match the original expression.** Addition is commutative, so:

```
sin²(x) + cos²(x) = cos²(x) + sin²(x) = 1
```

**Final answer:** `sin²(x) + cos²(x) = 1` for every value of `x`. This result is called the **Pythagorean identity**, and it holds universally because it is just the Pythagorean theorem applied to the unit circle.

   --param problem="integrate the square root of (4 minus x squared)" \
   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sage \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'integrate the square root of (4 minus x squared)' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 258 tokens, 18250ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1035 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1035 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  **We want to find ∫√(4 − x²) dx.**

In plain English: find the area function whose rate of change is the top half of a circle of radius 2.

**Step 1 — Choose a substitution.** The expression 4 − x² suggests letting **x = 2 sin θ**, so **dx = 2 cos θ dθ**.

**Step 2 — Simplify the square root.** Substituting: 4 − x² = 4 − 4 sin²θ = 4 cos²θ, so √(4 − x²) = **2 cos θ**.

**Step 3 — Rewrite the integral.** ∫ (2 cos θ)(2 cos θ dθ) = ∫ **4 cos²θ dθ**.

**Step 4 — Apply the half-angle identity** cos²θ = (1 + cos 2θ)/2: the integral becomes 4 · ∫(1 + cos 2θ)/2 dθ = **2∫(1 + cos 2θ) dθ**.

**Step 5 — Integrate term by term:** 2[θ + sin 2θ / 2] + C = **2θ + sin 2θ + C**.

**Step 6 — Expand sin 2θ = 2 sin θ cos θ**, giving **2θ + 2 sin θ cos θ + C**.

**Step 7 — Back-substitute** using sin θ = x/2 → θ = arcsin(x/2), and cos θ = √(4 − x²)/2:
- 2θ = 2 arcsin(x/2)
- 2 sin θ cos θ = 2 · (x/2) · √(4 − x²)/2 = x√(4 − x²)/2

**Final answer:**

$$\int \sqrt{4 - x^2}\, dx = \frac{x\sqrt{4-x^2}}{2} + 2\arcsin\!\left(\frac{x}{2}\right) + C$$

**Step 5 — Simplify using a trig identity (optional but clean).** Since sin²(x) = (1 − cos(2x))/2, we get sin²(x)/2 = (1 − cos(2x))/4, which can also be written −cos(2x)/4 + constant — the two forms differ only by the absorbed constant.

**Final answer:** ∫ sin(x) cos(x) dx = **sin²(x)/2 + C**, or equivalently **−cos(2x)/4 + C**.

   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sage \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the Laplace transform of exp(-2*t)' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 193 tokens, 9802ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (775 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 775 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  We want to find F(s) = L{e^{-2t}}, which means computing the integral of e^{-2t} multiplied by the Laplace kernel e^{-st} over t from 0 to ∞.

**Definition:** F(s) = ∫₀^∞ e^{-2t} · e^{-st} dt

**Step 1 — Combine the exponentials:** e^{-2t} · e^{-st} = e^{-(s+2)t}, so the integral becomes ∫₀^∞ e^{-(s+2)t} dt.

**Step 2 — Antiderivative:** The antiderivative of e^{-(s+2)t} with respect to t is -1/(s+2) · e^{-(s+2)t} (assuming s+2 ≠ 0).

**Step 3 — Evaluate at the upper limit (t → ∞):** Provided s > -2, the exponent -(s+2)t → -∞, so e^{-(s+2)t} → 0, giving 0.

**Step 4 — Evaluate at the lower limit (t = 0):** -1/(s+2) · e^{0} = -1/(s+2).

**Step 5 — Subtract (upper minus lower):** 0 - (-1/(s+2)) = 1/(s+2).

**Final answer:** L{e^{-2t}} = **1/(s+2)**, valid for s > -2.

## sonnet-4-6 (claude_cli) — backend=sage — solver=false — run 1

_Tier: T4 | Problem ID: `p018` | Host: `mac-wens-Mac-mini.local`_

```bash
(spl123) $ spl3 run cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl --kernel --llm claude_cli \
   --param problem="compute the symbolic sum of 1 over n squared from n equals 1 to infinity" \
   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sage \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'compute the symbolic sum of 1 over n squared from n equals 1 to infinity' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 343 tokens, 26913ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1373 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1373 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  **The Basel Problem**

The problem asks for the exact closed-form value of the infinite sum $\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \dfrac{1}{n^2} = 1 + \tfrac{1}{4} + \tfrac{1}{9} + \tfrac{1}{16} + \cdots$, which visibly converges but whose limit is not obvious. We follow Euler's 1734 approach, starting from the Taylor series $\dfrac{\sin x}{x} = 1 - \dfrac{x^2}{6} + \dfrac{x^4}{120} - \cdots$, so the coefficient of $x^2$ in this series is $\mathbf{-\tfrac{1}{6}}$. The zeros of $\sin x$ lie at $x = \pm\pi, \pm 2\pi, \pm 3\pi, \ldots$, so treating $\dfrac{\sin x}{x}$ as an "infinite polynomial" factored over all its roots gives the product representation $\dfrac{\sin x}{x} = \displaystyle\prod_{n=1}^{\infty}\!\left(1 - \dfrac{x^2}{n^2\pi^2}\right)$. Expanding that product, the only way to produce a single factor of $x^2$ is to take the $-\dfrac{x^2}{n^2\pi^2}$ term from exactly one parenthesis and "1" from every other, so the coefficient of $x^2$ in the product is $-\displaystyle\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}\dfrac{1}{n^2\pi^2}$. Both expressions represent the same function, so their $x^2$ coefficients must be equal: $-\displaystyle\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}\dfrac{1}{n^2\pi^2} = -\dfrac{1}{6}$. Multiplying both sides by $-\pi^2$ yields $\displaystyle\sum_{n=1}^{\infty}\dfrac{1}{n^2} = \dfrac{\pi^2}{6}$.

$$\boxed{\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^2} = \frac{\pi^2}{6} \approx 1.6449\ldots}$$
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 26914ms
Log:     /Users/mac-wen/.spl/logs/symbolic_math-claude_cli-claude-sonnet-4-6-20260620-122003.md

## sonnet-4-6 (claude_cli) — backend=sage — solver=true — run 1

**Step 3 — Real roots from linear factors:** x − 1 = 0 gives **x = 1**; x + 1 = 0 gives **x = −1**.

**Step 4 — Complex roots from quadratic factor:** x² + 1 = 0 means x² = −1, so x = ±√(−1) = **x = i** and **x = −i**.

**Final answer:** the four roots are **1, −1, i, −i**.

Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false

INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 231 tokens, 9562ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (926 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 926 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  We want to find all functions y(x) satisfying y'' - 3y' + 2y = 0 — a second-order, linear, homogeneous ODE with constant coefficients.

**Step 1 — Assume a solution of the form y = e^(rx).** Then y' = r·e^(rx) and y'' = r²·e^(rx).

**Step 2 — Substitute into the ODE.** We get r²e^(rx) - 3r·e^(rx) + 2e^(rx) = 0.

**Step 3 — Factor out e^(rx).** Since e^(rx) ≠ 0 for all x, we can divide through, leaving the **characteristic equation**: r² - 3r + 2 = 0.

**Step 4 — Factor the characteristic equation.** r² - 3r + 2 = (r - 1)(r - 2) = 0, giving two distinct real roots **r₁ = 1** and **r₂ = 2**.

**Step 5 — Build the general solution.** Because the roots are real and distinct, e^(r₁x) = e^x and e^(r₂x) = e^(2x) are two linearly independent solutions, and any linear combination is also a solution.

**General solution:**

> **y(x) = C₁eˣ + C₂e²ˣ**

where C₁ and C₂ are arbitrary constants determined by initial conditions.

[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'compute the inverse Laplace transform of s / (s**2 + 4), then verify by taking the Laplace transform of the result' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 235 tokens, 10996ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (942 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 942 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  **Problem:** Find the function of time whose Laplace transform is F(s) = s/(s² + 4), then check the answer by Laplace-transforming it back.

**Step 1 — Match to a standard form.** The table entry for cosine is L{cos(at)} = s/(s² + a²). Comparing s/(s² + 4) with s/(s² + a²), we read off a² = 4, so **a = 2**.

**Step 2 — Read off the inverse transform.** Because the form matches exactly with a = 2, the inverse Laplace transform is **f(t) = cos(2t)** (for t ≥ 0).

**Step 3 — Verify: compute L{cos(2t)} from the definition.** We need ∫₀^∞ cos(2t) e^(−st) dt. Writing cos(2t) = Re[e^(2it)], the integral becomes Re[∫₀^∞ e^(−(s−2i)t) dt] = Re[1/(s − 2i)]. Multiplying numerator and denominator by the conjugate: 1/(s − 2i) = (s + 2i)/((s)² + 4), so Re[(s + 2i)/(s² + 4)] = **s/(s² + 4)**.

**Step 4 — Conclusion.** The Laplace transform of cos(2t) is exactly s/(s² + 4), confirming our answer.

**Final answer:** L⁻¹{s/(s² + 4)} = **cos(2t)**.

Output:  Okay, let’s break down this partial fraction decomposition problem for 1 / (x² - 1). The goal is to express the rational function as a sum of simpler fractions with linear factors in the denominator. First, we factor the denominator: x² - 1 = (x - 1)(x + 1).  Next, we set up the partial fraction decomposition as follows: 1 / (x² - 1) = A/(x - 1) + B/(x + 1), where A and B are constants to be determined. To solve for A and B, we multiply both sides by (x - 1)(x + 1):  1 = A(x + 1) + B(x - 1). Now, let’s choose specific values of x. If x = 1, we get: 1 = A(1+1) + B(1-1) => 1 = 2A => A = 1/2.  If x = -1, we get: 1 = A(-1+1) + B(-1-1) => 1 = -2B => B = -1/2. Therefore, the partial fraction decomposition is (1/2)/(x - 1) + (-1/2)/(x + 1).

Final Answer:  (1/2)/(x - 1) - (1/2)/(x + 1)

   --param problem="find the limit of sin(x) divided by x as x approaches 0" \

   --param backend=sympy \

```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the limit of sin(x) divided by x as x approaches 0' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 186 tokens, 5819ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (593 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 593 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Okay, let’s tackle this limit problem! The student wants to know what happens to the expression sin(x) / x as *x* gets incredibly close to zero. We can use a clever trick involving the Squeeze Theorem (also known as the Sandwich Theorem). First, we know that -1 ≤ sin(x) ≤ 1 for all values of *x*.  Now, dividing by *x* (assuming *x* is approaching 0 but not actually 0), we get -1/x ≤ sin(x)/x ≤ 1/x. As *x* approaches 0, -1/x approaches infinity and 1/x approaches zero. Therefore, by the Squeeze Theorem, lim (x→0) sin(x)/x = 0.

So, the limit of sin(x) divided by x as x approaches 0 is 0.
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 5821ms

Step 3: Calculate the second derivative of sin(x): d^2/dx^2 [sin(x)] = -sin(x).

Step 9: Calculate the fifth derivative of sin(x): d^5/dx^5 [sin(x)] = cos(x).
Step 10: Evaluate the fifth derivative at x=0: cos(0) = 1.

Therefore, the Taylor series expansion of sin(x) around x = 0 up to degree 5 is:  sin(x) = x - (x^3)/3! + (x^5)/5!

Output:  Okay, let's tackle this integral! The problem asks us to find the indefinite integral of the function √(4 - x²). We can make a substitution: u = 4 - x², then du = -2x dx, so x dx = -1/2 du.  Substituting into the integral gives us ∫√u * (-1/2) du = -1/2 ∫ √u du. Now we can use the power rule for integration: ∫ u^n du = (u^(n+1))/(n+1) + C, so -1/2 ∫ u^(1/2) du = -1/2 * (u^(3/2))/(3/2) + C.  Simplifying this gives us -1/2 * (2/3) * u^(3/2) + C = -1/3 * u^(3/2) + C. Finally, substituting back u = 4 - x² yields -1/3 * (4 - x²)^(3/2) + C.

Therefore, the integral of √(4 - x²) is  -1/3 * (4 - x²)^(3/2) + C.

```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl

[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the eigenvalues of the 2 by 2 matrix with rows [1, 2] and [3, 4]' — solving with NO verifier

INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 320 tokens, 9538ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (788 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 788 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Okay, let's find the eigenvalues of the 2x2 matrix with rows [1, 2] and [3, 4]. First, we represent the matrix as:  [[1, 2], [3, 4]]. The characteristic equation is given by det(A - λI) = 0, where A is our matrix, λ represents the eigenvalues, and I is the identity matrix. Substituting, we get det([[1-λ, 2], [3, 4-λ]]) = (1-λ)(4-λ) - (2)(3) = 0. Expanding this, we have 4 - λ - 4λ + λ² - 6 = 0, which simplifies to λ² - 5λ - 2 = 0.  Now, we solve this quadratic equation for λ using the quadratic formula: λ = (-b ± √(b² - 4ac)) / 2a, where a=1, b=-5, and c=-2. Applying the formula, we get λ = (5 ± √((-5)² - 4 * 1 * -2)) / 2 = (5 ± √(25 + 8)) / 2 = (5 ± √33) / 2.  Therefore, the eigenvalues are λ₁ = (5 + √33)/2 and λ₂ = (5 - √33)/2.

The eigenvalues are (5 + √33)/2 and (5 - √33)/2.
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 9539ms
Log:     /Users/mac-wen/.spl/logs/symbolic_math-ollama-gemma3-20260620-122436.md
```

Status:  complete
Output:  Okay, let's find the Laplace transform of exp(-2t). The problem asks us to compute L{exp(-2t)}, where L{} denotes the Laplace transform operator. We start with the definition of the Laplace transform:  L{f(t)} = F(s) = ∫₀^∞ e^(-st) f(t) dt. In our case, f(t) = exp(-2t), so we have L{exp(-2t)} = ∫₀^∞ e^(-st) * e^(-2t) dt = ∫₀^∞ e^(-(s+2)t) dt. Now we can evaluate this integral:  F(s) = [ (-1/(s+2)) * e^(-(s+2)t) ] evaluated from 0 to ∞. Plugging in the limits of integration, we get F(s) =  (-1/(s+2)) * (lim┬(t→∞) e^(-(s+2)t) - e^0) = (-1/(s+2)) * (0 - 1) = 1/(s+2).

Therefore, the Laplace transform of exp(-2t) is 1/(s+2).

Output:  Okay, let's solve this differential equation! The problem asks us to find a function *y(x)* such that its derivative, *y'(x)*, is equal to itself, *y(x)*, and we know *y(0) = 1*.  This is a very simple first-order ordinary differential equation. First, we can rewrite the equation as *dy/dx = y*. Now, we can separate variables: *dy / y = dx*. Integrating both sides gives us *ln|y| = x + C*, where *C* is the constant of integration.  Exponentiating both sides yields *|y| = e^(x+C) = e^x * e^C*. Let's denote *A = ±e^C*, so we have *y = A*e^x*. Finally, applying the initial condition *y(0) = 1*, we get *1 = A*e⁰ = A*, so *A = 1*. Therefore, the solution is  *y(x) = e^x*.

However, there are four roots because this is a degree 4 polynomial. We also have *x* = -1 as a root since (-1)<sup>4</sup> = 1. Therefore, the roots of *x*<sup>4</sup> - 1 are: x = 1, x = -1, x = i, and x = -i.

Output:  Okay, let's solve this second-order linear ODE! The problem asks us to find a general solution for the equation y''(x) - 3y'(x) + 2y(x) = 0.  First, we assume a solution of the form y(x) = e^(rx), where 'r' is a constant. Then, we differentiate twice: y'(x) = re^(rx) and y''(x) = r^2e^(rx). Substituting these into the ODE gives r^2e^(rx) - 3re^(rx) + 2e^(rx) = 0. Factoring out e^(rx), we get e^(rx)(r^2 - 3r + 2) = 0.  Since e^(rx) is never zero, we solve the characteristic equation: r^2 - 3r + 2 = 0. This factors to (r-1)(r-2) = 0, so the roots are r_1 = 1 and r_2 = 2. Finally, the general solution is y(x) = c_1e^(x) + c_2e^(2x), where c_1 and c_2 are arbitrary constants.

Output:  Okay, let's tackle this inverse Laplace transform problem! The question asks us to find L⁻¹{s / (s² + 4)} and then verify our answer by taking the Laplace transform of the calculated inverse transform. First, we recall that the Laplace transform of sin(at) is a/((s^2 + a^2)^2).  We can rewrite s/(s² + 4) as: s = (1/2)(2s)/(s² + 4). Now, applying the Laplace Transform to 2s/(s² + 4), we get L⁻¹{2s / (s² + 4)} = 2 * L⁻¹{s / (s² + 4)} = 2sin(2t).  Therefore, L⁻¹{s / (s² + 4)} = (1/2) * 2sin(2t) = sin(2t). Finally, we can verify this by taking the Laplace transform of sin(2t), which is 2/(s² + 4).

So, the inverse Laplace transform of s / (s² + 4) is sin(2t).

Since directly substituting $x=0$ results in the indeterminate form $\frac{0}{0}$, we must use a method like L'Hôpital's Rule. First, we take the derivative of the numerator, $\sin(x)$, which gives us $\cos(x)$. Next, we take the derivative of the denominator, $x$, which gives us $1$. We then evaluate the limit of the new expression: $\lim_{x \to 0} \frac{\cos(x)}{1}$. Finally, substituting $x=0$ into this simplified expression gives us $\frac{\cos(0)}{1}$, which equals $\frac{1}{1}$.

The limit of $\frac{\sin(x)}{x}$ as $x$ approaches $0$ is 1.

First, we define the matrix $A$ as:
$$A = \begin{pmatrix} 1 & 2 \\ 3 & 4 \end{pmatrix}$$
To find the eigenvalues ($\lambda$), we must solve the characteristic equation $\det(A - \lambda I) = 0$.
We calculate $A - \lambda I$:
$$A - \lambda I = \begin{pmatrix} 1-\lambda & 2 \\ 3 & 4-\lambda \end{pmatrix}$$
Next, we find the determinant:
$$\det(A - \lambda I) = (1-\lambda)(4-\lambda) - (2)(3)$$
$$\det(A - \lambda I) = (4 - \lambda - 4\lambda + \lambda^2) - 6$$
$$\det(A - \lambda I) = \lambda^2 - 5\lambda - 2$$
Setting the determinant to zero gives us the characteristic equation: $\lambda^2 - 5\lambda - 2 = 0$.
Using the quadratic formula, we solve for $\lambda$:
$$\lambda = \frac{-(-5) \pm \sqrt{(-5)^2 - 4(1)(-2)}}{2(1)}$$
$$\lambda = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{25 + 8}}{2}$$
The eigenvalues of the matrix are $\lambda = \frac{5 \

Output:  This problem asks us to find the function $y(x)$ whose derivative, $y'(x)$, is equal to itself, given that the function's value at $x=0$ is 1.

We start by recognizing that the differential equation $y'(x) = y(x)$ can be rewritten as $\frac{dy}{dx} = y$.

Next, we separate the variables: $\frac{dy}{y} = dx$.

Integrating both sides yields $\ln|y| = x + C_1$, which means $y(x) = A e^x$, where $A$ is an arbitrary constant.

Now we use the initial condition $y(0)=1$ to find $A$: $1 = A e^0$, so $A=1$.

Therefore, the final solution to the differential equation with the given initial condition is $y(x) = e^x$.

Output:  The problem asks us to find the inverse Laplace transform of $\frac{s}{s^2 + 4}$ and then verify this result by taking the Laplace transform of the answer.

First, we compute the inverse Laplace transform using the known pair $\mathcal{L}^{-1}\left\{\frac{s}{s^2 + a^2}\right\} = \cos(at)$. Since $a^2=4$, we have $a=2$; therefore, the inverse transform is:
$$\mathcal{L}^{-1}\left\{\frac{s}{s^2 + 4}\right\} = \cos(2t)$$

Next, we verify this result by taking the Laplace transform of the derived function $\cos(2t)$. Using the property $\mathcal{L}\{\cos(at)\} = \frac{s}{s^2 + a^2}$, we substitute $a=2$:
$$\mathcal{L}\{\cos(2t)\} = \frac{s}{s^2 + 2^2} = \frac{s}{s^2 + 4}$$

Since the result of the second operation is the original function, the inverse transform is verified. The final answer for the inverse Laplace transform is $\cos(2t)$.

Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to find the derivative of the function \( f(x) = x^4 - 2x^2 + 1 \).

Let's break it down step by step:

1. **Step 1:** We start with \( f(x) = x^4 - 2x^2 + 1 \).
2. **Step 2:** To differentiate, we apply the power rule to each term. The power rule states that if you have a term of the form \( ax^n \), its derivative is \( n \cdot ax^{n-1} \).

   - For \( x^4 \): The derivative is \( 4x^{3} \).
   - For \( -2x^2 \): The derivative is \( -2 \cdot 2x^{1} = -4x \).
   - For the constant term \( 1 \): Its derivative is \( 0 \) (since the derivative of any constant is zero).

3. **Step 3:** Combining these results, we get the derivative \( f'(x) = 4x^3 - 4x + 0 \).

Therefore, the final answer is \( f'(x) = 4x^3 - 4x \).

Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to simplify the rational expression \(\frac{x^2 - 1}{x - 1}\).

Let's break it down step by step:

1. First, we recognize that \(x^2 - 1\) can be factored using the difference of squares formula: \(a^2 - b^2 = (a + b)(a - b)\). Here, \(a = x\) and \(b = 1\), so:
   \[
   x^2 - 1 = (x + 1)(x - 1)
   \]

2. Now we substitute this factored form back into the original expression:
   \[
   \frac{x^2 - 1}{x - 1} = \frac{(x + 1)(x - 1)}{x - 1}
   \]

3. We can now cancel out the common factor of \(x - 1\) in the numerator and the denominator, as long as \(x \neq 1\):
   \[
   \frac{(x + 1)(x - 1)}{x - 1} = x + 1
   \]

So, the simplified form of the expression is:
\[
x + 1
\]

Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to first expand the expression \((x+1)^2\) and then factor the expanded form.

Step 1: Expand \((x+1)^2\).
To do this, we use the formula for squaring a binomial: \((a+b)^2 = a^2 + 2ab + b^2\).
Here, \(a = x\) and \(b = 1\). So,
\[
(x+1)^2 = x^2 + 2 \cdot x \cdot 1 + 1^2 = x^2 + 2x + 1.
\]

Step 2: Factor the expanded form \(x^2 + 2x + 1\).
Notice that this is a perfect square trinomial. It can be factored as:
\[
x^2 + 2x + 1 = (x+1)(x+1) = (x+1)^2.
\]

The final answer is \((x+1)^2\).

   --param backend=sympy \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the partial fraction decomposition of 1 / (x**2 - 1)' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 514 tokens, 24332ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1212 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1212 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to decompose \( \frac{1}{x^2 - 1} \) into partial fractions.

Step 1: Factor the denominator.
\[ x^2 - 1 = (x + 1)(x - 1) \]
So, we want to write:
\[ \frac{1}{(x + 1)(x - 1)} = \frac{A}{x + 1} + \frac{B}{x - 1} \]

Step 2: Combine the fractions on the right side.
\[ \frac{1}{(x + 1)(x - 1)} = \frac{A(x - 1) + B(x + 1)}{(x + 1)(x - 1)} \]
Since the denominators are equal, we can set the numerators equal:
\[ 1 = A(x - 1) + B(x + 1) \]

Step 3: Expand and rearrange terms.
\[ 1 = Ax - A + Bx + B \]
\[ 1 = (A + B)x + (-A + B) \]

Step 4: Equate the coefficients of \( x \) and the constant term on both sides.
For the coefficient of \( x \):
\[ 0 = A + B \]
For the constant term:
\[ 1 = -A + B \]

Step 5: Solve the system of equations.
From \( 0 = A + B \), we get \( B = -A \).
Substitute \( B = -A \) into \( 1 = -A + B \):
\[ 1 = -A - A \]
\[ 1 = -2A \]
So, \( A = -\frac{1}{2} \).

Since \( B = -A \), we get \( B = \frac{1}{2} \).

Step 6: Substitute \( A \) and \( B \) back into the partial fractions.
\[ \frac{1}{(x + 1)(x - 1)} = \frac{-\frac{1}{2}}{x + 1} + \frac{\frac{1}{2}}{x - 1} \]

Final Answer:
\[ \frac{1}{x^2 - 1} = -\frac{1}{2(x + 1)} + \frac{1}{2(x - 1)} \]

## qwen2.5 (ollama) — backend=sympy — solver=false — run 1

_Tier: T2 | Problem ID: `p006` | Host: `mac-wens-Mac-mini.local`_

```bash
(spl123) $ spl3 run cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl --kernel --llm ollama:qwen2.5 \
   --param problem="find the limit of sin(x) divided by x as x approaches 0" \
   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sympy \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sympy solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the limit of sin(x) divided by x as x approaches 0' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 357 tokens, 17353ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1249 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1249 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to find the limit of \(\frac{\sin(x)}{x}\) as \(x\) approaches 0.

Let's approach this step by step:

1. Consider a small angle \(x\). We know that as \(x\) gets closer to 0, \(\sin(x)\) and \(x\) become very close in value.
2. To see why this is true, draw a unit circle and consider the triangle formed with an angle of size \(x\). The length of the opposite side (which approximates \(\sin(x)\)) will be almost equal to the arc length corresponding to \(x\) radians.
3. As \(x\) approaches 0, the ratio \(\frac{\sin(x)}{x}\) becomes exactly 1 because the sine and the angle in radians become essentially identical.
4. To make this more rigorous, we can use a geometric argument: In the limit as \(x\) gets very small, the area of the sector of the circle is approximately equal to \(\frac{x}{2}\), while the area of the triangle formed by the radius and the tangent line at the point (1,0) on the unit circle is approximately \(\frac{\sin(x)}{2}\).
5. Equating these areas as \(x\) approaches 0 gives us \(\frac{x}{2} = \frac{\sin(x)}{2}\), which simplifies to \(\sin(x) \approx x\).
6. Therefore, the ratio \(\frac{\sin(x)}{x}\) approaches 1.

So, the limit of \(\frac{\sin(x)}{x}\) as \(x\) approaches 0 is **1**.
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 17354ms
Log:     /Users/mac-wen/.spl/logs/symbolic_math-ollama-qwen2.5-20260620-123952.md
```

INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1334 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The student wants to expand the sine function, sin(x), as a Taylor series around x = 0, keeping terms up to degree 5.

To start, we need to recall that the general form of a Taylor series expansion is:

\[ f(x) = f(a) + \frac{f'(a)}{1!}(x-a) + \frac{f''(a)}{2!}(x-a)^2 + \frac{f'''(a)}{3!}(x-a)^3 + \cdots \]

For our case, \(a = 0\), so the series becomes:

\[ \sin(x) = \sin(0) + \frac{\cos(0)}{1!}x - \frac{\sin(0)}{2!}x^2 - \frac{\cos(0)}{3!}x^3 + \frac{\sin(0)}{4!}x^4 + \frac{\cos(0)}{5!}x^5 + \cdots \]

Now, let's calculate each term:

1. \( \sin(0) = 0 \)
2. \( \cos(0) = 1 \), so the first non-zero coefficient after the constant term is \( \frac{1}{1!}x = x \).
3. The second derivative of sin(x) at 0 is \( -\sin(0) = 0 \), but the next term involves the third derivative.
4. The third derivative of sin(x) is \( -\cos(0) = -1 \), so we have \( -\frac{1}{3!}x^3 = -\frac{x^3}{6} \).
5. The fourth derivative of sin(x) at 0 is \( \sin(0) = 0 \), but the next term involves the fifth derivative.
6. The fifth derivative of sin(x) is \( \cos(0) = 1 \), so we have \( \frac{1}{5!}x^5 = \frac{x^5}{120} \).

Putting it all together, and keeping only terms up to degree 5:

\[ \sin(x) \approx x - \frac{x^3}{6} + \frac{x^5}{120} \]

This is the Taylor series expansion of sin(x) around x = 0, keeping terms up to degree 5.

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking to find the integral of \(\sqrt{4 - x^2}\).

Let's break this down step by step:

1. **Identify the integral**: We need to integrate \(\int \sqrt{4 - x^2} \, dx\).
   
2. **Substitution**: Let \(x = 2\sin(\theta)\), then \(dx = 2\cos(\theta) d\theta\). The limits of integration will change based on the range of \(\theta\) corresponding to the original \(x\) values.

3. **Transform the integral**: Substitute \(x = 2\sin(\theta)\) and \(dx = 2\cos(\theta)d\theta\):
   \[
   \int \sqrt{4 - (2\sin(\theta))^2} \cdot 2\cos(\theta) d\theta = \int \sqrt{4(1 - \sin^2(\theta))} \cdot 2\cos(\theta) d\theta
   \]
   
4. **Simplify the integrand**: Use the Pythagorean identity \(1 - \sin^2(\theta) = \cos^2(\theta)\):
   \[
   \int \sqrt{4\cos^2(\theta)} \cdot 2\cos(\theta) d\theta = \int 2\cos(\theta) \cdot 2\cos(\theta) d\theta = 4 \int \cos^2(\theta) d\theta
   \]

5. **Integrate using a known formula**: Use the identity \(\cos^2(\theta) = \frac{1 + \cos(2\theta)}{2}\):
   \[
   4 \int \frac{1 + \cos(2\theta)}{2} d\theta = 2 \int (1 + \cos(2\theta)) d\theta
   \]

6. **Perform the integration**: Integrate term by term:
   \[
   2 \left[ \theta + \frac{\sin(2\theta)}{2} \right] + C = 2\theta + \sin(2\theta) + C
   \]
   
7. **Substitute back for \(x\)**: Recall that \(x = 2\sin(\theta)\), so \(\sin(\theta) = \frac{x}{2}\). Using the double-angle formula, \(\sin(2\theta) = 2\sin(\theta)\cos(\theta) = x\sqrt{1 - \left(\frac{x}{2}\right)^2} = x\sqrt{\frac{4 - x^2}{4}} = \frac{x\sqrt{4 - x^2}}{2}\):
   \[
   2\arcsin\left(\frac{x}{2}\right) + x\sqrt{1 - \left(\frac{x}{2}\right)^2} + C = 2\arcsin\left(\frac{x}{2}\right) + \frac{x\sqrt{4 - x^2}}{2} + C
   \]

The final answer is:
\[
\int \sqrt{4 - x^2} \, dx = 2\arcsin\left(\frac{x}{2}\right) + \frac{x\sqrt{4 - x^2}}{2} + C
\]

INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 374 tokens, 17989ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (926 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 926 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to find the integral of \(\sin(x) \times \cos(x)\), and then simplify the result.

Let's start by rewriting the integrand using a trigonometric identity:
\[ \sin(x) \cos(x) = \frac{1}{2} \sin(2x). \]
So, we need to find the integral of \(\frac{1}{2} \sin(2x)\):
\[ \int \frac{1}{2} \sin(2x) \, dx. \]

Next, take the constant factor outside the integral:
\[ \frac{1}{2} \int \sin(2x) \, dx. \]

Now, to integrate \(\sin(2x)\), we use a substitution. Let \(u = 2x\). Then \(du = 2 \, dx\) or \(dx = \frac{1}{2} du\):
\[ \frac{1}{2} \int \sin(u) \cdot \frac{1}{2} \, du = \frac{1}{4} \int \sin(u) \, du. \]

The integral of \(\sin(u)\) is \(-\cos(u)\), so we get:
\[ \frac{1}{4} (-\cos(u)) + C = -\frac{1}{4} \cos(u) + C. \]

Substitute back \(u = 2x\) to get the final result:
\[ -\frac{1}{4} \cos(2x) + C. \]

Thus, the integral of \(\sin(x) \times \cos(x)\) is:
\[ -\frac{1}{4} \cos(2x) + C. \]

   --param hostname="mac-wens-Mac-mini.local" \
   --param backend=sage \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl

[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the eigenvalues of the 2 by 2 matrix with rows [1, 2] and [3, 4]' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 563 tokens, 26671ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1421 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1421 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking for the eigenvalues of a 2x2 matrix with rows [1, 2] and [3, 4]. To find the eigenvalues, we need to solve the characteristic equation, which involves finding the determinant of the matrix \( A - \lambda I \), where \( A \) is our given matrix and \( I \) is the identity matrix.

The given matrix \( A \) is:
\[ A = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 2 \\ 3 & 4 \end{bmatrix} \]

The identity matrix \( I \) is:
\[ I = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{bmatrix} \]

So, \( A - \lambda I \) is:
\[ A - \lambda I = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 2 \\ 3 & 4 \end{bmatrix} - \lambda \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 1 \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} 1-\lambda & 2 \\ 3 & 4-\lambda \end{bmatrix} \]

The determinant of \( A - \lambda I \) is:
\[ \det(A - \lambda I) = (1-\lambda)(4-\lambda) - (2)(3) \]
\[ = (1-\lambda)(4-\lambda) - 6 \]
\[ = 4 - \lambda - 4\lambda + \lambda^2 - 6 \]
\[ = \lambda^2 - 5\lambda - 2 \]

To find the eigenvalues, we set this determinant equal to zero:
\[ \lambda^2 - 5\lambda - 2 = 0 \]

We solve this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula \( \lambda = \frac{-b \pm \sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} \), where \( a = 1 \), \( b = -5 \), and \( c = -2 \):
\[ \lambda = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{(-5)^2 - 4(1)(-2)}}{2(1)} \]
\[ = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{25 + 8}}{2} \]
\[ = \frac{5 \pm \sqrt{33}}{2} \]

So, the eigenvalues are:
\[ \lambda_1 = \frac{5 + \sqrt{33}}{2}, \quad \lambda_2 = \frac{5 - \sqrt{33}}{2} \]

   --param problem="find the Laplace transform of exp(-2*t)" \

   --param backend=sage \
   --param enable_solver=false
```

```output
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 2 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/symbolic_math.spl
INFO:spl.registry:Registry: loaded 0 workflow(s) from cookbook/77_neurosymbolic/sympolic_tools.spl
Registry: ['neurosymbolic_solver', 'solve_chain_step']
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
IPython kernel: enabled (name=python3, scope=session, timeout=60.0s)
INFO:spl.executor:IPython kernel registered: CALL run_python(@code) INTO @result
Running workflow: neurosymbolic_solver(['problem', 'hostname', 'backend', 'enable_solver', 'model'])
[INFO] [neurosymbolic_solver] host=mac-wens-Mac-mini.local backend=sage solver=false
[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the Laplace transform of exp(-2*t)' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 373 tokens, 17935ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (903 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 903 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking for the Laplace transform of \( e^{-2t} \).

To find this, we'll use the definition of the Laplace transform:
\[ \mathcal{L}\{f(t)\} = F(s) = \int_0^\infty e^{-st} f(t) dt. \]

For \( f(t) = e^{-2t} \), we substitute into the formula:
\[ \mathcal{L}\{e^{-2t}\} = \int_0^\infty e^{-st} e^{-2t} dt = \int_0^\infty e^{-(s+2)t} dt. \]

Now, let's solve this integral:
\[ \int_0^\infty e^{-(s+2)t} dt. \]

We can factor out the constant \(-(s+2)\) from the exponent and recognize that we are integrating an exponential function:
\[ \frac{1}{-(s+2)} \int_0^\infty e^{-t(s+2)} dt = -\frac{1}{s+2} \left[ -\frac{e^{-(s+2)t}}{s+2} \right]_0^\infty. \]

Evaluating the integral at the limits:
\[ -\frac{1}{-(s+2)^2} \left( 0 - e^{-0(s+2)} \right) = \frac{1}{(s+2)^2} (1 - 0). \]

Thus, we get:
\[ \mathcal{L}\{e^{-2t}\} = \frac{1}{(s+2)}. \]

So the final answer is: \(\frac{1}{s+2}\).

INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1391 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to find a function \( y(x) \) that satisfies the differential equation \( y'(x) = y(x) \) with the initial condition \( y(0) = 1 \).

Let's solve this step by step:

1. First, we recognize that the given differential equation \( y'(x) = y(x) \) is a first-order linear ordinary differential equation.
2. To solve it, we can use separation of variables. We rewrite the equation as:
   \[
   \frac{dy}{dx} = y
   \]
3. Next, we separate the variables by dividing both sides by \( y \) and multiplying both sides by \( dx \):
   \[
   \frac{1}{y} dy = dx
   \]
4. Now, we integrate both sides of the equation:
   \[
   \int \frac{1}{y} dy = \int dx
   \]
5. The integral on the left side is \( \ln|y| + C_1 \), and the integral on the right side is \( x + C_2 \):
   \[
   \ln|y| = x + C
   \]
   where \( C = C_2 - C_1 \) is a constant.
6. To solve for \( y \), we exponentiate both sides to remove the natural logarithm:
   \[
   |y| = e^{x + C} = e^C \cdot e^x
   \]
7. Since \( e^C \) is just another constant, let's denote it by \( A \):
   \[
   y(x) = Ae^x
   \]
8. Finally, we use the initial condition \( y(0) = 1 \) to find the value of \( A \). Substituting \( x = 0 \) and \( y = 1 \) into the equation:
   \[
   1 = Ae^0 = A
   \]
9. Therefore, we have \( A = 1 \), so the solution is:
   \[
   y(x) = e^x
   \]

The final answer is \( y(x) = e^x \).
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 22562ms

\[ \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^2} \]

1. **Identify the Series**: The series is \( 1 + \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{9} + \frac{1}{16} + \cdots \).

2. **Recognize the Series Type**: This series is known as the Basel problem, and its sum has been proven to be \( \frac{\pi^2}{6} \). However, let's derive it step-by-step for clarity.

3. **Express the Partial Sum**: The partial sum of the first few terms can be written as:

\[ S_N = 1 + \frac{1}{4} + \frac{1}{9} + \cdots + \frac{1}{N^2} \]

4. **Consider the Infinite Series**: As \( N \) approaches infinity, we are looking for the limit of \( S_N \).

5. **Use Known Result from Analysis**: The sum of this infinite series has been proven to converge to a specific value:

\[ \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^2} = \frac{\pi^2}{6} \]

This result is derived using advanced techniques such as Fourier series or the residue theorem, but for our purposes, we accept this known mathematical fact.

The final answer is:

\[ \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^2} = \frac{\pi^2}{6} \]

Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to find all roots of the equation \(x^4 - 1 = 0\). This means we need to solve for \(x\) in this equation.

Let's start by factoring the left side of the equation:
\[ x^4 - 1 = (x^2)^2 - 1^2 \]

We recognize this as a difference of squares, so it can be factored further:
\[ (x^2 - 1)(x^2 + 1) = 0 \]

Now, we solve each factor separately. First, for \(x^2 - 1 = 0\):
\[ x^2 - 1 = (x - 1)(x + 1) = 0 \]
This gives us:
\[ x - 1 = 0 \quad \text{or} \quad x + 1 = 0 \]
So, the solutions are:
\[ x = 1 \quad \text{and} \quad x = -1 \]

Next, for \(x^2 + 1 = 0\):
\[ x^2 + 1 = 0 \]
This can be rewritten as:
\[ x^2 = -1 \]
Taking the square root of both sides gives us:
\[ x = \pm i \]
where \(i\) is the imaginary unit.

Combining all these, we have the roots:
\[ x = 1, -1, i, -i \]

So, the final answer is: The roots of the equation \(x^4 - 1 = 0\) are \(1\), \(-1\), \(i\), and \(-i\).

Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to find the general solution to the second-order ordinary differential equation \( y''(x) - 3y'(x) + 2y(x) = 0 \).

To solve this, we'll first assume a solution of the form \( y(x) = e^{rx} \). Substituting this into the ODE gives us:

1. The derivative \( y'(x) = re^{rx} \)
2. The second derivative \( y''(x) = r^2e^{rx} \)

Substituting these into the original equation, we get:
\[ r^2e^{rx} - 3re^{rx} + 2e^{rx} = 0 \]

Since \( e^{rx} \neq 0 \), we can divide through by \( e^{rx} \) to obtain the characteristic equation:
\[ r^2 - 3r + 2 = 0 \]

Now, let's solve this quadratic equation for \( r \):
\[ r^2 - 3r + 2 = (r - 1)(r - 2) = 0 \]
So, we get two roots: \( r_1 = 1 \) and \( r_2 = 2 \).

Since the roots are real and distinct, the general solution to the ODE is given by:
\[ y(x) = C_1e^{r_1x} + C_2e^{r_2x} \]
where \( C_1 \) and \( C_2 \) are arbitrary constants.

Therefore, the final answer is:
\[ y(x) = C_1e^x + C_2e^{2x} \]

INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 378 tokens, 18112ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1209 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1209 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  Sure! The problem is asking us to find the inverse Laplace transform of \( \frac{s}{s^2 + 4} \) and then verify it by taking the Laplace transform of the result.

1. **Step 1: Identify the form**  
   We recognize that \( \frac{s}{s^2 + 4} \) resembles a standard form in Laplace transforms, specifically the derivative form. The inverse Laplace transform of \( \frac{\omega s}{s^2 + \omega^2} \) is \( \cos(\omega t) \). Here, \( \omega = 2 \).

2. **Step 2: Apply the known inverse Laplace transform**  
   Therefore, \( \mathcal{L}^{-1}\left\{\frac{s}{s^2 + 4}\right\} = \cos(2t) \).

3. **Step 3: Verify by taking the Laplace transform of the result**  
   Now we take the Laplace transform of \( \cos(2t) \). The Laplace transform of \( \cos(\omega t) \) is given by \( \frac{s}{s^2 + \omega^2} \).

4. **Step 4: Substitute \( \omega = 2 \)**  
   So, the Laplace transform of \( \cos(2t) \) is \( \frac{s}{s^2 + 4} \).

5. **Final Answer**  
   The inverse Laplace transform of \( \frac{s}{s^2 + 4} \) is \( \cos(2t) \), and verifying by taking the Laplace transform confirms this result.

That's it! We've completed the problem step-by-step, starting from identifying the form to confirming our answer.

Output:  The student has asked to differentiate the expression \(x^4 - 2x^2 + 1\) with respect to \(x\). Here's how we can approach this step-by-step, showing our reasoning and derivation at each stage.

Step 1: Recognize that differentiation is a process of finding the rate of change (the derivative) of a function as you vary its input value. This expression given has three terms \(x^4\), \(-2x^2\), and \(1\). Each term needs to be differentiated separately, keeping in mind the power rule which states that for any real number n, d/dx(x^n) = n*x^(n-1).

Step 2: Apply the power rule. The first term becomes \(4x^{4 - 1} = 4x^3\). For the second term \(-2x^2\), we have \((2)(-2)x^{2 - 1} = -4x^1\) or simply \(-4x\). Lastly, for the constant in the expression (\(+1\)), its derivative is zero since a constant's rate of change with respect to \(x\) is always zero.

Step 3: Add these results together according to the sum rule of differentiation—which states that the derivative of two functions added or subtracted together is just the sum or difference of their derivatives. In this case, we add up each pair of resulting terms from step 2 (e.g., \(4x^3 + (-4x)\)).

Step 4: Simplify to get a final result. The derivative simplifies down to \(4x^3 - 4x\). This is our answer as it represents the rate at which changes occur in this function when varying \(x\).

Output:  To simplify the rational expression \(\frac{x^2 - 1}{x - 1}\), first observe that \(x^2 - 1\) is in factored form, which suggests using a difference of squares to potentially cancel out terms. The difference of squares identity states \((a + b)(a - b) = (a - b)^2\), where \(a = x\) and \(b = 1\). Applying this rule gives:

\[
(x^2 - 1) = (x + 1)(x - 1).
\]

Substituting the factored form back into the original expression, we have:

\[
\frac{x^2 - 1}{x - 1} = \frac{(x + 1)(x - 1)}{x - 1}.
\]

Since \(x - 1\) is a common factor in both the numerator and the denominator, it can be canceled out:

\[
\frac{x^2 - 1}{x - 1} = (x + 1).
\]

This final expression \((x + 1)\) states that our rational expression simplifies to \(x + 1\). Thus, the simplified form of \(\frac{(x^2 - 1)}{x - 1}\) is indeed:

\[
\boxed{x + 1}.
\]

Output:  The problem asks to differentiate \( f(x)=3x^3-x \) and then factor if necessary, followed by solving for \( x \). Here's how we can approach this step by step:

1. **Differentiation**: To find the derivative of \( f(x) = 3x^3 - x \), use the power rule which states that the derivative of \( x \) raised to the power \( n \) is \( nx^{n-1} \). Starting with the first term, \( (3x^3)' = 3 \cdot 3x^{3-1} = 9x^2 \), and for the second term, \((-x)' = -1\). So, differentiating \( f(x) \) gives us:
   \[ f'(x) = 9x^2 - 1 \]

2. **Factorization**: Since differentiation is applied on each term separately, no factorization was necessary in this step. The derivative remains as-is without factoring it further because the polynomial didn't simplify through grouping or other common factor methods.

3. **Solving for \( x \), if possible**: To solve for \( x \) when \( f'(x) = 0 \), we need to set up and solve an equation with this condition in mind:
   \[ 9x^2 - 1 = 0 \]
   This is obtained by setting the derivative equal to zero. Rearranging, we get:
   \[ 9x^2 = 1 \]
   Dividing both sides by 9 gives \( x^2 = \frac{1}{9} \). Taking square roots of both sides yields two solutions for \( x \) (since squaring any real number twice will cancel out the operation):
   \[ x = \pm\sqrt{\frac{1}{9}} \]
   Simplifying these expressions, we find that:
   \[ x = \pm \frac{1}{3} \]

4. **Final Answer**: The derivative of \( f(x) = 3x^3 - x \) is \( f'(x) = 9x^2 - 1 \), and solving for the possible values of \( x \) gives us two solutions: \( x = +\frac{1}{3} \) or \( -\frac{1}{3} \).

The result is \(9\) when we solve for \(x=0\).

Thus, \( B = ? \).

Output:  To differentiate \(e^x\), we will use the power rule, which states that if f(x) = e^x then its derivative is f'(x) = e^x. Here's how it works step by step:

1. **Power Rule Basics**: The power rule for differentiation tells us that if you have a function like \(f(x) = x^n\), the derivative will be \(f'(x) = n \cdot x^{n-1}\). This is because as we differentiate, each term loses one power by becoming its coefficient times the lower exponent.

2. **Applying Power Rule to e^x**: Now consider our function \(e^x\). When you expand it using the power rule (which for this base case gives 1), you see that each time you differentiate x, its power decreases: differentiating once more gives a term of "x", and then zero when differentiated one last time.

3. **Deriving f'(x) = e^x**: Thus, applying the power rule directly to \(e^x\) results in \(f'(x) = 1 \cdot x^{1-1} = x\). However, remember that this is just a general result from calculus; specifically for base \(e\), we know its derivative must be precisely what was derived above.

4. **Simplifying and Finalizing the Derivative**: The final step involves recognizing that since our function \(f(x) = e^x\) has already been differentiated to give us f'(x) = e^x, there is no further simplification needed for this specific case. Thus, we have confirmed analytically what calculus often tells us by rote: the derivative of \(e^x\) with respect to x is simply \(e^x\).
  
This step-by-step approach ensures that all calculations are correct and transparent, providing a clear explanation of how derivatives work in practice.

INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (1219 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1218 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  The problem asks us to find the limit of \(\frac{\sin(x)}{x}\) as \(x\) approaches 0. In simpler terms, we are looking for what happens to the ratio of sine of an angle (in radians) and that same angle when the angle gets very small but not equal to zero.

To start with, let's recall the definition of sine in a circle: \(\sin(x) = y\) where \(x\) is the angle between the positive x-axis and the line segment connecting (0, 0) and (r, y). Here, r is the radius of the circle.

Now, for small angles (\(x\) approaching zero), we can use a very useful approximation: \(\sin(x) \approx x\). This comes from the fact that sine values are highest at 0 and decrease linearly as \(x\) increases (from 0 to some angle).

Substituting this into our expression gives us \(\frac{x}{\sin(x)} \approx \frac{x}{x} = 1\), for very small \(x\). Here, we used the properties of limits: when multiplying or dividing a function by another that approaches 1 under certain conditions, the limit remains close to the original.

Therefore, as x approaches zero from the right (positive side), \(\frac{\sin(x)}{x}\) also approaches one. This is our final answer: the limit of \(\frac{\sin(x)}{x}\) as \(x\) approaches 0 from the right is 1.

INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 1230 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sympy, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  The problem asks for the Taylor series expansion of the sine function, sin(x), around x=0 up to degree 5 (sixth-degree polynomial). This means we need to express sin(x) as a sum of powers of x multiplied by coefficients that come from taking derivatives and plugging them back in at various points.

To start, let's recall the first few derivatives of sin(x):
sin'(x) = cos(x), 
sin''(x) = -sin(x), 
sin'''(x) = -cos(x), 
and so on.

Now we can plug these derivatives back into x, replacing each derivative with its corresponding power of x and coefficient from the original sin(x):
1st term: sin(0) * x^0 = sin(0) * 1 = 0 (since sin(0)=0),
2nd term: cos(0) * (-x)^1 = 1 * -x = -x,
3rd term: -sin(0) * (x^2)/2! = -0 * x^2/2 = 0 (since the coefficient for the second derivative is zero),
4th term: -cos(0) * (-x)^3/3! = -1 * -x^3/6,
5th term: sin(0) * (x^4)/4! = 0 * x^4/24, and finally;
6th term: cos(0) * (-x)^5/5! = 1 * x^5 / 120.

We then sum these terms up to keep only the first six (up to degree five in total), including their coefficients: sin(x) ~ -x + x^3/6 - x^5/120.

This final series, with all steps included, provides a Taylor polynomial expansion of sine function around x=0 keeping terms up to degree 5 inclusive.

   \[ \frac{{\rm d}u}{{\rm d}x} = -2x. \]
2. Next, substitute \(dx = -\frac{{\rm d}x}{2x}\) into the original integral:

5. Substituting back into the integral:
   \[ \int{-\frac{{\rm d}}{{\sqrt{u}}}\left(\frac{{\rm d}x}{2x}\right)} = -\frac{1}{2}\cdot\int{\sqrt{u}\,{\rm d}x}. \]
6. Now, integrate \(u\) with respect to \(x\):
   \[ \int{-\frac{1}{2}\cdot\left(\frac{{\rm d}}{{\rm d}x}\left(-\frac{1}{2}{\sqrt{u}}\right)\right)\,{\rm d}x}. \] Integrate:
7. Combine the terms and simplify to find \(-\frac{1}{4}\ln|u|+C\), where \(C\) is a constant of integration. Then substitute back in \(x\):
   \[ -\frac{1}{4}\ln|4-x^2| + C. \]

Substituting sin(x) for du in the original integral gives:
\[ \int\limits_u^1 {v\,dv} \]

\[ \int\limits_u^1 {v\,dv} \]

Output:  The problem asks for finding the eigenvalues of a specific 2x2 matrix given in its row form, with elements [1, 2] and [3, 4]. This task involves determining these special values that are used to describe how the transformation by this matrix will affect vectors. Here's how you can find it:

1. **Matrix Definition**: The given matrix is a 2x2 matrix where its elements are [1, 2] for the first row and [3, 4] for the second row. This defines the matrix A.

2. **Determine Matrix Elements**: From the rows of A, we see that the main diagonal has an eigenvalue λ = (first element on diag) - (second element on diag) which is equal to:
   \[ \lambda_1 = 1 - 4 = -3 \]
   This value comes from subtracting twice the second entry in each row.

3. **Calculate Other Eigenvalue**: An alternative way involves finding the determinant of matrix A minus λ times the identity matrix, I (where I is simply a diagonal matrix with all elements being 1). In this case, it gives us:
   \[ \lambda_2 = det(A - λI) = det\begin{pmatrix} \lambda & -λ \\ -λ & λ-3 \end{pmatrix} = (\lambda - 3)^2 \]

4. **Simplify Eigenvalue Calculation**: To find the actual eigenvalues, we substitute a general eigenvalue (let's choose k for simplicity) into our expressions:
   \[ λ_1(k) = 1 - k = -3 \Rightarrow k_1 = 4 \quad\text{and} \]
   \[ λ_2(k) = k^2 - 5k + 6 = (k-3)(k-2) \]

5. **Evaluate the Eigenvalues at k**: By setting \( k \) equal to specific values, we can find the eigenvalues:
   For \( k_1 \), set \( k = 4 \) and get \( λ_1(4) = -3 \); for \( k_2 \), set \( k = 2 \) or \( k = 3 \) and find each gives \( λ_2(2) = 0 \) and \( λ_2(3) = 1 \).

6. **Final Answer**: The eigenvalues of the matrix are -3, 0, and 1. These values indicate how much each eigenvector will be stretched or shrunk when multiplied by this matrix, which is crucial for understanding its effects on vectors in linear algebra applications such as transforming signals or solving systems of equations.

[INFO] [arm=llm_only] Problem 'find the Laplace transform of exp(-2*t)' — solving with NO verifier
INFO:httpx:HTTP Request: POST http://localhost:11434/v1/chat/completions "HTTP/1.1 200 OK"
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE segment 1 (solve_directly) -> 670 tokens, 13611ms
INFO:spl.executor:GENERATE chain done -> @result (2238 chars total)
INFO:spl.executor:RETURN: 2237 chars | status=complete, arm=llm_only, backend=sage, steps=0

Status:  complete
Output:  To find the Laplace transform of \( e^{-2t} \), let's start by defining what we mean by a "Laplace transform". The Laplace transform is an integral transform widely used to solve differential equations, which transforms a function of a real variable (often time) into a function of a complex variable. It maps any given function \( f(t) \) from the real numbers into another function:

\[ \mathcal{L}\{f\}(s) = F(s) = \int_0^\infty e^{-st} f(t) \, dt \]

The rule to find Laplace transforms is essentially based on properties of integration and exponential functions. In our case, we want the transform of \( e^{-2t} \), so:

\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \int_0^\infty e^{-st} e^{-2t} \, dt \]

Simplify this by combining terms inside the integral and notice that it is now an exponential function times another exponential, which simplifies to a constant. The integration limits stay \( 0 \) to \( \infty \), so:
 last step here
\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \int_0^\infty e^{(-s-2)t} \, dt \]

Now we notice that this is an exponential decay function, which often appears in Laplace transforms. The integral of such a function from \( 0 \) to \( \infty \) with respect to the variable of integration (in our case, t) will be convergent because it does not oscillate and decays as \( t \) goes to infinity.

Therefore, by properties of limits under the integral sign:
\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \lim_{T \to \infty} \int_0^T e^{(-s-2)t} \, dt \]

Evaluate this limit by substitution \( u = T + t \) and \( du = dt \):
\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \lim_{T \to \infty} \int_0^{T+T} e^{(-s-2)(u-T)} \, du \]
Simplify:
\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \lim_{T \to \infty} \int_0^2 e^{(-s-(u-T))}\, du \]

Notice that the integral does not depend on \( T \) anymore as we are now only integrating from 0 to a constant upper limit of integration (which goes to infinity). The Laplace transform is then:
\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \int_0^\infty e^{(-st - u)}\, du \]
Simplify the exponential part by absorbing \( s \) and combining constants inside the integral:
\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \int_0^\infty e^{(-(s+1)u)}\, du \]
This is now a standard Laplace transform of an exponential function. Using properties of integration, we know that this converges to:
\[ L\{e^{-2t}\}(s) = \frac{1}{s + 1} \]
LLM calls: 1  Latency: 13614ms

Status:  complete
Output:  The problem we're addressing is a first-order ordinary differential equation (ODE): \(y'(x) = y(x)\) with an initial condition \(y(0) = 1\). This means, in English, that the derivative of some function \(y\) equals itself at every point on the x-axis. We need to solve this ODE and find a specific expression for \(y(x)\).

Let's start by separating variables: rewrite the equation as \(\frac{dy}{dx} = y\). Now, integrate both sides with respect to \(x\): \[\int \frac{dy}{y} = \int dx.\] This gives us \(\ln|y| = x + C\), where \(C\) is an arbitrary constant.

To solve for the relationship between \(y\) and \(x\), we take the exponential of both sides, which brings back a minus sign since it's negative on one side: \[y(x) = Ce^x.\] Now that we have our solution in terms of this unknown constant \(C\), we plug in the initial condition to solve for \(C\): \[1 = C e^0 \implies 1 = C,\] so \(C=1\).

Thus, the final solution is: \[y(x) = e^x.\] This simple result indicates that every point on the x-axis (and thus everywhere in its domain) maps to an output value of \(e^x\) under this function.

Output:  The problem asks for the sum of an infinite series where each term is \( \frac{1}{n^2} \) and we are asked to find this sum from 1 to infinity, starting at n = 1. This can be represented symbolically as:

\[ S = \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^夕 \]

To begin solving this problem, let's first calculate the partial sums for some finite number of terms N. For example, \( S_N = \sum_{n=1}^{N} \frac{1}{n^2} \). Notice that as you add more and more terms (i.e., increase N), the sum gets closer to a particular value which we will denote by T:

\[ T = \lim_{N\to\infty} S_N \]

The series \( \sum_{n=1}^{N} \frac{1}{n^2} \) can be simplified using techniques similar to telescoping series. Notice that each term in the sum is one less than a factor of n, which means that most terms will cancel out when we combine them pairwise:

\[ S_N = 1 - \frac{1}{2^2} + \frac{1}{2^2} - \frac{1}{3^2} + \cdots + \frac{1}{(N-1)^2} - \frac{1}{N^2} \]

Now, we can combine these terms to form pairs that sum up to zero:

\[ S_N = 1 - \frac{1}{N^2} \]

Taking the limit as N approaches infinity gives us our final answer:

\[ T = \lim_{N\to\infty} (1 - \frac{1}{N^2}) = 1 \]

So, the sum of \( \sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^2} \) from n equals 1 to infinity is simply equal to 1.

The remaining factored form of \(x^4 - 1\) in terms of these simpler polynomials is then \((x - 1)(x^3 + x^2 + x + 1)\). This expression has all real roots because the other factors simplify to constants, and thus we have expressed our result correctly.
